Administrative Details


1. We will provide the full text of evidence upon request but will only post first-and-last words on the wiki for organizational reasons. If there is something that we haven't posted, please email us and we will post it for you.

2. We prefer not to answer questions during pre-debate preparation time about the arguments we have previously read in debates. We will do our best to maintain a complete list of the arguments we have read in the 1NC and extended in the 2NR in each of our debates—that list is below under "Specific Negative Strategies". Before asking us a pre-round question, please consult the wiki. We will reciprocate this request: we will not ask for this information from the teams we are debating, either.

3. For affirmative cites, please email Rahim Shakoor (rkshakoor@gmail.com). For negative cites, please email Jason Sigalos (jnsigalos@gmail.com).

Affirmative


1ACs


1AC — Greenhill


Contention One: The Status Quo

First—withdrawal from Afghanistan will be slow and small regardless of conditions on the ground.
Paul R. Pillar , Visiting Professor and Director of the Security Studies Program in the Edmund A. Walsh School of Foreign Service at Georgetown University, served in the Central Intelligence Agency for 28 years, 2010 (“What Conditions, Exactly?,” Paul Pillar’s Blog at The National Interest, August 15th, Available Online at http://nationalinterest.org/print/blog/conditions-3872 , Accessed 08-23-2010)
In his interview on Meet the Press [3], ... situation and discourse about the war back home.

Second—the current strategy is doomed to failure—prerequisites for success are non-existent.
The Afghanistan Study Group —an ad hoc group of public policy practitioners, former U.S. government officials, academics, business representatives, policy-concerned activists and association leaders concerned with the Obama administration’s policy course in Afghanistan,2010 (“What’s Wrong With The Current U.S Strategy,” A New Way Forward: Rethinking U.S. Strategy in Afghanistan, August 16th, Available Online at http://www.afghanistanstudygroup.org/NewWayForward_report.pdf , Accessed 08-29-2010)
Sixth, our military strategy is failing because the prerequisites ... maintain than the Afghan government can afford.

Contention Two: Imperial Overstretch

The current COIN strategy is failing—it is distracting the military and hurting the economy.
Richard N. Haass , President of the Council on Foreign Relations, 2010 (“We’re Not Winning. It’s Not Worth It.,” Newsweek, July 18th, Available Online at http://www.newsweek.com/2010/07/18/we-re-not-winning-it-s-not-worth-it.print.html , Accessed 08-30-2010)
The decisions that flowed from this were equally contradictory ... commitment to begin to bring troops home next year.

We will isolate two impacts—Subpoint A is Military Power:

COIN is devastating U.S. global leadership—it overstretches our forces, decimates our diplomatic leverage, and trades off with defense modernization.
Robert Haddick , Managing Editor of Small Wars Journal, former Marine Corps Officer, holds a B.A. and M.B.A. from the University of Illinois,2010 (“This Week at War: The Afghanistan Vortex,” Foreign Policy, June 25th, Available Online athttp://www.foreignpolicy.com/articles/2010/06/25/this_week_at_war_the_afghanistan_vortex , Accessed 08-31-2010)
Afghanistan is becoming a deepening vortex for both the ... across the world and the United States will be paying them for years to come.

This crushes military readiness—maintaining COIN posture in Afghanistan dooms U.S. military power.
Gian P. Gentile , Assistant Professor of History and Director of the Military History Program at the United States Military Academy, Lieutenant Colonel in the U.S. Army who has served two tours of duty in Iraq, Visiting Fellow at the Council on Foreign Relations, 2010(“Freeing the Army from the Counterinsurgency Straightjacket,” Joint Force Quarterly, Issue 58, July, Available Online athttp://ndu.edu/press/counterinsurgency-straightjacket.html , Accessed 08-31-2010)
Carl von Clausewitz said that a center of gravity is something to be discovered ... Perhaps the Army needs to find the time to do it now.

And—strong military power is key to deter the rise of hostile global rivals—maintaining a superiority gap is key.
Kim R. Holmes , Ph.D., Vice President for Foreign and Defense Policy Studies and Director of the Kathryn and Shelby Cullom Davis Institute for International Studies at The Heritage Foundation, 2009 (“The Perils of Prediction and Unpreparedness in Building the Future Force,” Heritage Foundation Lecture #1131, September 2nd, Available Online at http://www.heritage.org/Research/NationalSecurity/hl1131.cfm , Accessed 10-27-2009)
I think the underlying assumption here about the nature of our ... programs because we think we have "run up the score."

Independently—COIN distracts U.S. strategic focus—it sets the wrong priorities.
Christopher Layne , Robert M. Gates Chair in Intelligence and National Security at the Bush School of Government and Public Service at Texas A&M University, 2010 (“Petraeus' dubious strategy in Afghanistan,” The Chicago Tribune, August 23rd, Available Online athttp://www.chicagotribune.com/news/ct-oped-0823-afghanistan-20100823,0,3405676,print.story , Accessed 08-30-2010)
On its own terms, COIN is a problematic policy ... futile Eurasian land wars in places like Afghanistan and Iraq.

Specifically—an exclusive focus on Islamic terrorism has diverted America’s focus and weakened its global power.
George Friedman , internationally recognized expert in security and intelligence issues relating to national security, information warfare and computer security, Founder, Chairman, and Chief Intelligence Officer of STRATFOR—a private intelligence company that provides customized intelligence services for its clients, holds a Ph.D. in Government from Cornell University, 2010 (“9/11 and the 9-Year War,” Republished from Stratfor by RealClearPolitics, September 8th, Available Online at http://www.realclearworld.com/articles/2010/09/08/911_and_the_9-year_war_99164.html , Accessed 09-08-2010)
The most significant effect of 9/11 was that it knocked the ... create significant potential challenges to the United States.

This decimates our military power and risks the rise of hostile global rivals.
Daniel Goure , Vice President of the Lexington Institute, former Director of the Office of Strategic Competitiveness in the Office of the Secretary of Defense, former Deputy Director of the International Security Program at the Center for Strategic and International Studies, holds an M.A. and Ph.D. International Relations and Russian Studies from Johns Hopkins University and a B.A. in Government and History from Pomona College, 2010 (“Future Defense Budget Choices Require Clear Strategic Priorities,” Early Warning Blog—the official blog of the Lexington Institute, September 3rd, Available Online at http://www.lexingtoninstitute.org/future-defense-budget-choices-require-clear-strategic-priorities?a=1&c=1171 , Accessed 09-08-2010)
The United States cannot afford its national security strategy ... to air and sea power, outer space and cyber space.

Prioritize this impact—great power conflict is the only existential risk.
Paul K. Chisholm , foreign policy and international relations scholar based in Kenya, holds a University Degree in Sociology and Diploma in Human Resources, 2005 (“Terrorism, the Threat in Perspective and Great Power Conflict,” Free World Syndicate, April, Available Online athttp://www.synd.org/opinion-columns-chisholm/terrorism.html , Accessed 09-08-2010)
We've heard much about terrorism since the 9/11 attacks ... a full scale arms race and potential conflicts between nuclear parties.

And—some degree of strategic retrenchment is inevitable—the only question is whether the U.S. can maintain global stability.
Lawrence J. Korb , Senior Fellow at the Center for American Progress, Senior Advisor to the Center for Defense Information, Adjunct Professor of National Security Studies at Georgetown University, and Loren Thompson , Ph.D., Defense Analyst and Chief Operating Officer at the Lexington Institute, former Deputy Director of the Security Studies Program at Georgetown University, 2010 (“The U.S. can't afford unilateral military moves abroad,” Los Angeles Times, August 18th, Available Online at http://articles.latimes.com/2010/aug/18/opinion/la-oe-korb-partners-20100818/ , Accessed 09-08-2010)
The big question for policymakers is not whether defense spending ... needs to take so that partners can take on roles now beyond America's means.

Unfortunately—the current strategy in Afghanistan bogs the U.S. down and constrains the effectiveness of its power.
George Friedman , internationally recognized expert in security and intelligence issues relating to national security, information warfare and computer security, Founder, Chairman, and Chief Intelligence Officer of STRATFOR—a private intelligence company that provides customized intelligence services for its clients, holds a Ph.D. in Government from Cornell University, 2010 (“9/11 and the 9-Year War,” Republished from Stratfor by RealClearPolitics, September 8th, Available Online at http://www.realclearworld.com/articles/2010/09/08/911_and_the_9-year_war_99164.html , Accessed 09-08-2010)
The initial response was understandable and necessary ... global power can return to managing all of its global interests, along with the war on al Qaeda.

Subpoint B: Economic Power

COIN operations are sapping U.S. economic strength—the risk of fiscal collapse is high.
Gabriel Lajeunesse , Associate at the Institute for the Study of Diplomacy at Georgetown University, former Special Agent and Middle East/North Africa Area Specialist with the Air Force Office of Special Investigations, M.A. in Middle Eastern Studies from the Naval Postgraduate School and J.D. from Georgetown University Law Center, 2010 (“Towards a Fiscally and Politically Solvent Counterterrorism Strategy,” The Huffington Post, August 17th, Available Online at http://www.huffingtonpost.com/gabriel-lajeunesse/towards-a-fiscally-and-po_b_685127.html?view=print , Accessed 08-25-2010)
While such a conclusion has merit, COIN operations are tremendously costly ... cannot afford such costly long term commitments.

This guts U.S. global leadership—economic power is crucial to hegemony.
The Afghanistan Study Group —an ad hoc group of public policy practitioners, former U.S. government officials, academics, business representatives, policy-concerned activists and association leaders concerned with the Obama administration’s policy course in Afghanistan,2010 (“America’s Interests,” A New Way Forward: Rethinking U.S. Strategy in Afghanistan, August 16th, Available Online athttp://www.afghanistanstudygroup.org/NewWayForward_report.pdf , Accessed 08-29-2010)
The central goal of U.S. foreign and defense policy is to ensure ... especially when the costs of military engagement exceed the likely benefits.

This accesses every major impact—remaining bogged down in Afghanistan jeopardizes all U.S. foreign policy initiatives.
The Afghanistan Study Group —an ad hoc group of public policy practitioners, former U.S. government officials, academics, business representatives, policy-concerned activists and association leaders concerned with the Obama administration’s policy course in Afghanistan,2010 (“What’s Wrong With The Current U.S Strategy,” A New Way Forward: Rethinking U.S. Strategy in Afghanistan, August 16th, Available Online at http://www.afghanistanstudygroup.org/NewWayForward_report.pdf , Accessed 08-29-2010)
Finally, the rising costs of the war in Afghanistan also include opportunity costs. The war in Afghanistan has already consumed a considerable amount of President ... interests requires a fundamentally different approach.

And—failing to reign-in imperial overstretch risks global conflicts—strategic retrenchment is key to maintain U.S. power.
Christopher Layne , Associate Professor in the Bush School of Government and Public Service at Texas A&M University and Research Fellow with the Center on Peace and Liberty at The Independent Institute, 2006 (“The Unipolar Illusion Revisited: The Coming End of the United States' Unipolar Moment," International Security, Volume 31, Number 2, Fall, Available Online to Subscribing Institutions via Project Muse)
The United States has a hegemony problem because it wields hegemonic power ... in multipolar systems. Unilateral offshore balancing, indeed, is America's next grand strategy.

This is the controlling impact—U.S. leadership is unsustainable unless the U.S. transitions away from COIN and refocuses its foreign policy toward managing great power competition.
Christopher Layne , Robert M. Gates Chair in Intelligence and National Security at the Bush School of Government and Public Service at Texas A&M University, 2010 (“Graceful Decline: The End of Pax Americana,” The American Conservative, May 1st, Available Online athttp://amconmag.com/article/2010/may/01/00030/ , Accessed 09-01-2010)
The United States emerged from World War II ... we first need to change the way we see the world.

Finally—the best case scenario for the current strategy in Afghanistan is even worse than outright failure—staying the course is delusional.
Tom Engelhardt , Fellow at The Nation Institute, author of TomDispatch, Co-founder of the American Empire Project, 2010 (“Tomgram: Engelhardt, The Petraeus Syndrome,” TomDispatch, July 11th, Available Online at http://www.tomdispatch.com/dialogs/print/?id=175272 , Accessed 08-31-2010)
Okay, it hasn’t happened yet -- and the odds are it never will ... who needs a future Petraeus Syndrome?

Thus, Woodward Academy offers the following plan:

The United States federal government should substantially draw down its military presence in Afghanistan by shifting from a counter-insurgency strategy to a counter-terrorism strategy that reduces its military footprint to the minimum level needed to help train Afghan security forces, prevent massive human rights atrocities, resist an expansion of Taliban control beyond the Pashtun south, and engage in robust counter-terrorism operations as needed including a decrease to 68,000 troops or less by October 2011 and 30,000 or less by July 2012.

We’ll clarify.

Contention Three: Solvency

First—the plan is a substantial reduction in military presence—this saves billions and reduces resentment.
The Afghanistan Study Group —an ad hoc group of public policy practitioners, former U.S. government officials, academics, business representatives, policy-concerned activists and association leaders concerned with the Obama administration’s policy course in Afghanistan,2010 (“The Way Forward: A Five-Point Approach,” A New Way Forward: Rethinking U.S. Strategy in Afghanistan, August 16th, Available Online at http://www.afghanistanstudygroup.org/NewWayForward_report.pdf , Accessed 08-29-2010)
2. Scale back and eventually Suspend Combat operations in the South ... resentment at our large and intrusive military presence.

Second—shifting to a counter-terrorism posture is key to defeat al Qaeda—the status quo strategy is doomed to failure.
Austin Long , Assistant Professor of International and Public Affairs at Columbia University, co-founder of the Working Group on Insurgency and Irregular Warfare at the MIT Center for International Studies and participant in the RAND Counterinsurgency Board of Experts, 2010(“Small is Beautiful: The Counterterrorism Option in Afghanistan,” Orbis, Volume 54, Issue 2, Available Online to Subscribing Institutions via ScienceDirect)
One of the most important debates to emerge over the future of U.S. strategy in ... alignment of resources with goals, the essence of strategy.

Third—shifting to counter-terrorism is the most effective strategy—it reduces casualties, reassures allies, and keeps the pressure on extremists.
Thomas Snodgrass , Retired Colonel in the U.S. Air Force, Director of Military Affairs for The Society of Americans for National Existence, Former Adjunct Professor of History at Embry-Riddle Aeronautical University, 2010 (“Conservatives: Abandoning Counterinsurgency,” The American Thinker, August 5th, Available Online at http://www.americanthinker.com/2010/08/conservatives_abandoning_count.html , Accessed 08-30-2010)
The lesson learned from history must be to do what we know to be doable ... abandoned as inconspicuously and quickly as possible.

Fourth—the plan is key to prevent precipitous withdrawal—public support makes a long-term counter-insurgency posture politically untenable.
Steven Simon , Adjunct Senior Fellow for Middle Eastern Studies at the Council on Foreign Relations and former Director for Global Issues and Senior Director for Transnational Threats at the National Security Council, and Jonathan Stevenson , Professor of Strategic Studies at the U.S. Naval War College, 2009 (“Afghanistan: How Much is Enough?,” Survival, Volume 51, Issue 5, Available Online to Subscribing Institutions via InformaWorld)
It follows that the most difficult challenge to sustaining a maximalist ... United States will lose considerable traction in the region.

Finally—pessimistic predictions about the consequences of a drawdown are wrong—we’ll beat their disads with this card alone.
The Afghanistan Study Group —an ad hoc group of public policy practitioners, former U.S. government officials, academics, business representatives, policy-concerned activists and association leaders concerned with the Obama administration’s policy course in Afghanistan,2010 (“What’s Wrong With The Current U.S Strategy,” A New Way Forward: Rethinking U.S. Strategy in Afghanistan, August 16th, Available Online at http://www.afghanistanstudygroup.org/NewWayForward_report.pdf , Accessed 08-29-2010)
Second, a U.S. drawdown would not make Al Qaeda substantially ... military engagement will not significantly increase the threat from Al Qaeda.

1AC — St. Mark's


Contention One: The Status Quo

First, the situation in Afghanistan continues to deteriorate—election fraud, failed offensives, bank collapse, and increased casualty levels demonstrate the failure of current U.S. strategy.
Stephen M. Walt, Robert and Rene Belfer Professor of International Relations at Harvard University, 09-14-2010 (“Defending the Afghan Study Group Report,” Stephen Walt’s Foreign Policy Blog, September 14th, Available Online at http://walt.foreignpolicy.com/posts/2010/09/14/defending_the_afghan_study_group_report, Accessed 10-02-2010)
It is of course possible that Exum will one day be proven right, but one would have more faith in his judgment if the situation in Afghanistan had not gone from bad to worse since Obama took his advice. … Given the actual situation on the ground (and the condition of the U.S. economy), the Study Group concluded that it did not make sense to spend $100 billion or more per year trying to "nation-build" in a country whose entire GNP is about $14 billion.

Second, COIN is doomed by its inherent complexity—we’ll enter the accompanying PowerPoint slide into the record of the debate—it speaks for itself.
Charles V. Peña, Senior Fellow at the Independent Institute, Senior Fellow with the Coalition for a Realistic Foreign Policy, former Senior Fellow with the George Washington University Homeland Security Policy Institute and Former Director of Defense Policy Studies at the Cato Institute, Adviser to the Straus Military Reform Project, Analyst for MSNBC television, holds an M.A. in Security Studies from George Washington University, 05-23-2010 (“Why We Can’t Win in Afghanistan,” Antiwar.com, May 23rd, Available Online at http://original.antiwar.com/pena/2010/05/23/why-we-cant-win-in-afghanistan/print/, Accessed 10-01-2010)
Finally, there is this: … And – like a kaleidoscope – a small change to one thing changes everything else unpredictably and a completely new pattern emerges.

Contention Two: Military Power

First, COIN is devastating the U.S. Armed Forces—it overstretches the Army, decimates diplomatic leverage, and trades off with defense rehabilitation.
Robert Haddick, Managing Editor of Small Wars Journal, former Marine Corps Officer, holds a B.A. and M.B.A. from the University of Illinois, 05-25-2010 (“This Week at War: The Afghanistan Vortex,” Foreign Policy, June 25th, Available Online at http://www.foreignpolicy.com/articles/2010/06/25/this_week_at_war_the_afghanistan_vortex, Accessed 08-31-2010)
Afghanistan is becoming a deepening vortex for both the United States military and for the country's national security policies. … The costs stretch across the world and the United States will be paying them for years to come.

Second, COIN crushes military readiness—maintaining the current posture in Afghanistan dooms U.S. military power.
Gian P. Gentile, Assistant Professor of History and Director of the Military History Program at the United States Military Academy, Lieutenant Colonel in the U.S. Army who has served two tours of duty in Iraq, Visiting Fellow at the Council on Foreign Relations, 07-01-2010 (“Freeing the Army from the Counterinsurgency Straightjacket,” Joint Force Quarterly, Issue 58, July, Available Online at http://ndu.edu/press/counterinsurgency-straightjacket.html, Accessed 08-31-2010)
Carl von Clausewitz said that a center of gravity is something to be discovered, and it could vary depending on the aims of the war being fought.3 … Seriously debating and challenging current operational doctrine is hard while fighting a war at the same time, but it is not impossible—and it is an absolute necessity. Perhaps the Army needs to find the time to do it now.

Third, this risks the rise of hostile global rivals—attempts at “full spectrum dominance” are hollowing out the military and preventing the U.S. from meeting rising challenges.
Daniel Goure, Vice President of the Lexington Institute, former Director of the Office of Strategic Competitiveness in the Office of the Secretary of Defense, former Deputy Director of the International Security Program at the Center for Strategic and International Studies, holds an M.A. and Ph.D. International Relations and Russian Studies from Johns Hopkins University and a B.A. in Government and History from Pomona College, 09-03-2010 (“Future Defense Budget Choices Require Clear Strategic Priorities,” Early Warning Blog—the official blog of the Lexington Institute, September 3rd, Available Online at http://www.lexingtoninstitute.org/future-defense-budget-choices-require-clear-strategic-priorities?a=1&c=1171, Accessed 09-08-2010)
The United States cannot afford its national security strategy. … The consequence of this means focusing more on capabilities related to air and sea power, outer space and cyber space.

Independently, COIN narrows the gap between the U.S. and its peer competitors—this negates the benefits of strong military power.
Bernard I. Finel, Professor of Military Strategy and Operations at the U.S. National War College, former Executive Director of the Security Studies Program and the Center for Peace and Security Studies at Georgetown University, 02-01-2010 (“An alternative to COIN,” Armed Forces Journal, February Available Online at http://www.afji.com/2010/02/4387134, Accessed 10-12-2010)
The U.S. military is a dominant fighting force, capable of rapid global power projection and able to defeat state adversaries quickly and at relatively low cost in American lives and treasure. … Asymmetric “threats” are self-limited and should be largely ignored, and by doing so, the U.S. could free itself to apply military power more effectively in the types of conflict where American forces excel.

Finally, prioritize this impact—great power conflict is the only existential risk.
Paul K. Chisholm, foreign policy and international relations scholar based in Kenya, holds a University Degree in Sociology and Diploma in Human Resources, 2005 (“Terrorism, the Threat in Perspective and Great Power Conflict,” Free World Syndicate, April, Available Online at http://www.synd.org/opinion-columns-chisholm/terrorism.html, Accessed 09-08-2010)
We've heard much about terrorism since the 9/11 attacks. … Diplomacy should be redirected to head off a full scale arms race and potential conflicts between nuclear parties.

Contention Three: 9/11 Syndrome

First, the current strategy in Afghanistan bogs the U.S. down and constrains the effectiveness of its power.
George Friedman, internationally recognized expert in security and intelligence issues relating to national security, information warfare and computer security, Founder, Chairman, and Chief Intelligence Officer of STRATFOR—a private intelligence company that provides customized intelligence services for its clients, holds a Ph.D. in Government from Cornell University, 09-08-2010 (“9/11 and the 9-Year War,” Republished from Stratfor by RealClearPolitics, September 8th, Available Online at http://www.realclearworld.com/articles/2010/09/08/911_and_the_9-year_war_99164.html, Accessed 09-08-2010)
The initial response was understandable and necessary. … After nine years, the issue is not what to do in Afghanistan but how the global power can return to managing all of its global interests, along with the war on al Qaeda.

Second, this prevents the U.S. from addressing grand strategic challenges that pose existential risks—drawing down in Afghanistan is necessary to refocus foreign policy.
Kanti Bajpai, Professor of International Studies at Jawaharlal Nehru University (India), Professor in the Politics and International Relations of South Asia at both the School of Interdisciplinary Area Studies and the Department of Politics and International Relations at the University of Oxford (England), 09-13-2010 (“Exit Is A Smarter Strategy,” The Times of India, September 13th, Available Online at http://timesofindia.indiatimes.com/articleshow/6541291.cms?prtpage=1, Accessed 10-01-2010)
The problem with the present US course is that the workings of the US political and economic system, its role in East Asia and issues of the global commons are being neglected. … It must find its way back to these grand strategic challenges and not lose the woods for the trees.

Finally, this accesses every major impact—remaining bogged down in Afghanistan jeopardizes all U.S. foreign policy initiatives.
The Afghanistan Study Group—an ad hoc group of public policy practitioners, former U.S. government officials, academics, business representatives, policy-concerned activists and association leaders concerned with the Obama administration’s policy course in Afghanistan, 08-16-2010 (“What’s Wrong With The Current U.S Strategy,” A New Way Forward: Rethinking U.S. Strategy in Afghanistan, August 16th, Available Online at http://www.afghanistanstudygroup.org/NewWayForward_report.pdf, Accessed 08-29-2010)
Finally, the rising costs of the war in Afghanistan also include opportunity costs. … Protecting our vital interests requires a fundamentally different approach.

Contention Four: Civilian Control

First, Obama has caved to blackmail from military leaders—a crisis in civil-military relations is brewing in the status quo.
Michael A. Cohen, former Senior Research Fellow at the New America Foundation, B.A. in International Relations from American University and M.A. in International Relations from Columbia University, served in the Department of State as chief speechwriter for U.S. Representative to the United Nations Bill Richardson and Undersecretary of State Stuart Eizenstat, currently serves on the board of the National Security Network and has taught at Columbia University’s School of International and Public Affairs, 09-28-2010 (“Our Ongoing Civil-Military Relations Crisis,” Democracy Arsenal—an international relations blog published by the Center for American Progress and the Century Foundation's Security and Peace Initiative, September 28th, Available Online at http://progressiverealist.org/blogpost/our-ongoing-civil-military-relations-crisis, Accessed 10-12-2010)
Today's gobsmacking quote from the new Woodward book. … That's a crisis folks.

Second, Obama’s deference to Petraeus devastates civilian control of the military—the President, not the generals, should determine U.S. Afghanistan strategy.
Paul R. Pillar, Visiting Professor and Director of the Security Studies Program in the Edmund A. Walsh School of Foreign Service at Georgetown University, served in the Central Intelligence Agency for 28 years, 09-16-2010 (“Civil-Military Relations and Decision-making on Afghanistan,” Paul Pillar’s Blog at The National Interest, September 16th, Available Online at http://nationalinterest.org/print/blog/paul-pillar/civil-military-relations-decision-making-afghanistan-4089, Accessed 10-02-2010)
A feature article [3] in the New York Times points to a significant deficiency in the process through which the Obama administration will make its next major decisions about the war in Afghanistan, although the journalists don't call it a deficiency. … It also will be contrary to the American tradition of civilian supremacy over the military.

Third, now is the key time for Obama to reassert control and reject the Petraeus Lobby’s pleas for more time to continue COIN—this is vital to reassure allies and restore America’s international credibility.
Andrew J. Bacevich, Professor of History and International Relations at Boston University, graduated from West Point, former Colonel in the United States Army, holds a Ph.D. in American Diplomatic History from Princeton University, 10-05-2010 (“Obama Must Defy His Generals,” The New Republic, October 5th, Available Online at http://www.npr.org/templates/story/story.php?storyId=130346439, Accessed 10-12-2010)
"The President is an elected king," wrote Randolph Bourne nearly a century ago, "but the fact that he is elected has proved to be of far less significance ... than the fact that he is pragmatically a king." … Should he squander this last opportunity, however, the king is likely to find himself soon thereafter seeking new employment.

This is also a big deal for another reason: maintaining civilian control over the military is key to separation of powers, freedom, and democracy.
Richard H. Kohn, Professor of History and Chairman of the Curriculum in Peace, War, and Defense at the University of North Carolina at Chapel Hill, Executive Secretary of the Triangle Institute for Security Studies, and member of the Editorial Advisory Board of American Diplomacy, 1997 (“An Essay on Civilian Control of the Military,” American Diplomacy, Available Online at http://www.unc.edu/depts/diplomat/AD_Issues/amdipl_3/ kohn.html, Accessed 05-02-2008)
For democracy, civilian control – that is, control of the military by civilian officials elected by the people – is fundamental. … While a country may have civilian control of the military without democracy, it cannot have democracy without civilian control.

The impact is widespread oppression and violence—history proves.
Lynne Henderson, Professor of Law at the Indiana University School of Law at Bloomington, 1991 (“Authoritarianism and the Rule of Law,” Indiana Law Journal (66 Ind. L.J. 379), Spring, Available Online to Subscribing Institutions via Lexis-Nexis)
Substantive authoritarianism means opposition to the "liberal" values of tolerance of ambiguity and difference, insistence on obedience to rules, insistence on conformity, and use of coercion and punishment to ensure that obedience. … Other examples include the government largely ignoring oppression of and violence against African-American women, n22 and a long history of governmental tolerance of private oppression of women and children through violence. n23

Finally, evaluate this impact like a nuclear war—the risks are too high to justify.
Martin H. Redish, Professor of Law and Public Policy at Northwestern University, and Elizabeth J. Cisar, Law Clerk to Chief Judge William Bauer of the United States Court of Appeals, 1991 (“Constitutional Perspectives: Article: ‘If Angels Were To Govern’: The Need for Pragmatic Formalism In Separation of Powers Theory,” Duke Law Journal, December, Available Online to Subscribing Institutions via Lexis-Nexis, p. 449)
In summary, no defender of separation of powers can prove with certitude that, but for the existence of separation of powers, tyranny would be the inevitable outcome. … For just as in the case of the threat of nuclear war, no one wants to be forced into the position of saying, "I told you so."

Thus, Woodward Academy offers the following plan:

The President of the United States should direct the Department of Defense to substantially draw down the United States military presence in Afghanistan by shifting from a counter-insurgency strategy to a counter-terrorism strategy including a decrease to 68,000 troops or less by October 2011 and 30,000 or less by July 2012.

We’ll clarify.

Contention Five: Solvency

First, the plan is a substantial reduction in military presence—this saves billions and reduces resentment.
The Afghanistan Study Group—an ad hoc group of public policy practitioners, former U.S. government officials, academics, business representatives, policy-concerned activists and association leaders concerned with the Obama administration’s policy course in Afghanistan, 08-16-2010 (“The Way Forward: A Five-Point Approach,” A New Way Forward: Rethinking U.S. Strategy in Afghanistan, August 16th, Available Online at http://www.afghanistanstudygroup.org/NewWayForward_report.pdf, Accessed 08-29-2010)
2. Scale back and eventually Suspend Combat operations in the South and Reduce the U.S. Military footprint. … This step would save the U.S. at least $60 billion to $80 billion per year and reduce local resentment at our large and intrusive military presence.

Second, the plan enables a shift to more effective counter-terrorism operations—reducing military presence is key.
The Afghanistan Study Group—an ad hoc group of public policy practitioners, former U.S. government officials, academics, business representatives, policy-concerned activists and association leaders concerned with the Obama administration’s policy course in Afghanistan, 08-16-2010 (“The Way Forward: A Five-Point Approach,” A New Way Forward: Rethinking U.S. Strategy in Afghanistan, August 16th, Available Online at http://www.afghanistanstudygroup.org/NewWayForward_report.pdf, Accessed 08-29-2010)
3. Keep the focus on Al Qaeda and Domestic Security. … The Study Group also believes that more effort should be made to exploit potential cleavages among different radical groups in the region, a goal that would be facilitated as the U.S. military presence declines.

Third. shifting to counter-terrorism is the most effective strategy—it reduces casualties, reassures allies, and keeps the pressure on extremists.
Thomas Snodgrass, Retired Colonel in the U.S. Air Force, Director of Military Affairs for The Society of Americans for National Existence, Former Adjunct Professor of History at Embry-Riddle Aeronautical University, 08-05-2010 (“Conservatives: Abandoning Counterinsurgency,” The American Thinker, August 5th, Available Online at http://www.americanthinker.com/2010/08/conservatives_abandoning_count.html, Accessed 08-30-2010)
The lesson learned from history must be to do what we know to be doable and to avoid what we have learned is not. … A "stable" Afghan government under Hamid Karzai is a losing proposition that should be carefully abandoned as inconspicuously and quickly as possible.

Fourth, their disadvantages are terminally non-unique—counterterrorism operations are increasing in the status quo and even the neg hacks are losing faith in COIN.
Fred Kaplan, National Security Columnist for Slate, holds a Ph.D. in Political Science from the Massachusetts Institute of Technology, 10-13-2010 (“A New Plan for Afghanistan,” Slate, October 13th, Available Online at http://www.slate.com/toolbar.aspx?action=print&id=2270971, Accessed 10-14-2010)
Officials say a shift in U.S. war strategy has begun to take place in Afghanistan, away from classic counterinsurgency (protecting the population, providing basic services, promoting good government) and toward the traditional business of killing and capturing bad guys. … However, it is now calculated, even by many COIN advocates, that this process would take too long—and be too corrupted by Afghan politics—to work in any practical sense.

Fifth, the plan is key to prevent precipitous withdrawal—public support makes a long-term counter-insurgency posture politically untenable.
Steven Simon, Adjunct Senior Fellow for Middle Eastern Studies at the Council on Foreign Relations and former Director for Global Issues and Senior Director for Transnational Threats at the National Security Council, and Jonathan Stevenson, Professor of Strategic Studies at the U.S. Naval War College, 2009 (“Afghanistan: How Much is Enough?,” Survival, Volume 51, Issue 5, Available Online to Subscribing Institutions via InformaWorld)
It follows that the most difficult challenge to sustaining a maximalist US policy, leaving aside substantive questions of strategy, is that of keeping the American people on board. … If, on the other hand, the Obama administration promises more than it can deliver in Afghanistan, a reprise of Vietnam may occur: once failure becomes clear, domestic support will evaporate, the administration will be compelled to withdraw precipitously, and the United States will lose considerable traction in the region.

Finally, the best case scenario for the current strategy in Afghanistan is even worse than outright failure—staying the course is delusional.
Tom Engelhardt, Fellow at The Nation Institute, author of TomDispatch, Co-founder of the American Empire Project, 07-11-2010 (“Tomgram: Engelhardt, The Petraeus Syndrome,” TomDispatch, July 11th, Available Online at http://www.tomdispatch.com/dialogs/print/?id=175272, Accessed 08-31-2010)
Okay, it hasn’t happened yet -- and the odds are it never will. … With everything else Americans have to deal with, who needs a future Petraeus Syndrome?

Contention One is Adventurism

First, we’re not buying what the Obama Administration is selling: the war in Afghanistan is a colossal failure and evidence to the contrary is military fiction.
Michael A. Cohen, former Senior Research Fellow at the New America Foundation, B.A. in International Relations from American University and M.A. in International Relations from Columbia University, served in the Department of State as chief speechwriter for U.S. Representative to the United Nations Bill Richardson and Undersecretary of State Stuart Eizenstat, currently serves on the board of the National Security Network and has taught at Columbia University’s School of International and Public Affairs, 12-29-2010 (“The White House's Growing 'Credibility Gap' on Afghanistan,” Democracy Arsenal—an international relations blog published by the Center for American Progress and the Century Foundation's Security and Peace Initiative, December 29th, Available Online at http://progressiverealist.org/blogpost/white-houses-growing-credibility-gap-afghanistan, Accessed 01-03-2011)
A couple of months ago I wrote an article for Foreign Policy that argued only the US military is optimistic about the situation in Afghanistan. … To be blunt, it's becoming increasingly difficult to take anything the White House or the military says about the war in Afghanistan seriously.

Second, the campaign to mislead the American people requires us to challenge official orthodoxy—only rejecting the Obama Administration’s Afghanistan pathology can prevent the expansion of military control over U.S. foreign policy.
Stephen M. Walt, Robert and Rene Belfer Professor of International Relations at Harvard University, 2011 (“Where Do Bad Ideas Come From?,” Foreign Policy, January/February, Available Online at http://www.foreignpolicy.com/articles/2011/01/02/where_do_bad_ideas_come_from?print=yes&hidecomments=yes&page=full, Accessed 01-03-2011)
In theory, democracies like the United States should have a built-in advantage. … Some did express such qualms, of course, but doing so loudly and persistently was a good way to find oneself excluded from the political mainstream and certainly from the highest corridors of power.

Third, usurpation of grand strategy by the military brass will have devastating consequences—military dominance of foreign policy results in tunnel vision, conflict escalation, and adventurist wars that drag the U.S. into perpetual quagmires.
Eric Martin, editor of Obsidian Wings, The Progressive Realist, and American Footprints—three leading foreign policy blogs, holds a B.A. in Philosophy from New York University and a J.D. from Fordham University, 10-09-2010 (“If All You Have Is A Hammer…,” American Footprints—a foreign policy blog, October 9th, Available Online at http://americanfootprints.com/wp/2010/10/if-all-you-ask-is-a-hammer/, Accessed 10-19-2010)
One of the maladies plaguing US foreign policy creation is the over-reliance on, and undue deference shown to, the military when shaping that policy. … Given the ascendancy of the military, it should come as no surprise the extent to which conventional wisdom has hardened around the preferred narrative of the “can-do” crowd – as we are treated to optimistic assessments of what the military can achieve in a given setting, tales of turned corners, new hope, “the next six months,” deus ex machina like doctrine, mythically endowed “surges” etc.

Fourth, The impact is widespread conflict and suffering—military activism makes every impact worse.
Jeffrey D. Sachs, Director of The Earth Institute, Quetelet Professor of Sustainable Development, and Professor of Health Policy and Management at Columbia University, Special Advisor to United Nations Secretary-General Ban Ki-moon, former Director of the UN Millennium Project and Special Advisor to United Nations Secretary-General Kofi Annan on the Millennium Development Goals, widely considered to be the leading international economic advisor of his generation, holds a Ph.D. from Harvard University, 2007 (“America’s Failed Militarized Foreign Policy,” Project Syndicate, November 22nd, Available Online at http://www.project-syndicate.org/commentary/sachs135/English, Accessed 11-18-2010)
Many of today’s war zones – including Afghanistan, Ethiopia, Iran, Iraq, Pakistan, Somalia, and Sudan – share basic problems that lie at the root of their conflicts. … And we are all mortal.”

Finally, leaving authority for foreign policy in the hands of an unaccountable executive branch results in groupthink and irrational decision-making—this risks nuclear war and extinction.
David Gray Adler, Professor of Political Science at Idaho State University, 1996 (“The Judiciary and Presidential Power In Foreign Affairs: A Critique,” Perspectives on Law and the Public Interest (1 Persp. on L. & Pub. Int. 1), Fall, Available Online to Subscribing Institutions via Hein Online, p. 1-9)
{4} The Constitution envisions the conduct of foreign policy as a partnership between the President and Congress. … We turn now to an examination of the judiciary's contribution to executive hegemony in foreign affairs.

Contention Two is the Economy

First, COIN operations in Afghanistan are sapping U.S. economic strength—the risk of fiscal collapse is high.
Gabriel Lajeunesse, Associate at the Institute for the Study of Diplomacy at Georgetown University, former Special Agent and Middle East/North Africa Area Specialist with the Air Force Office of Special Investigations, M.A. in Middle Eastern Studies from the Naval Postgraduate School and J.D. from Georgetown University Law Center, 8-17-2010 (“Towards a Fiscally and Politically Solvent Counterterrorism Strategy,” The Huffington Post, August 17th, Available Online at http://www.huffingtonpost.com/gabriel-lajeunesse/towards-a-fiscally-and-po_b_685127.html?view=print, Accessed 08-25-2010)
While such a conclusion has merit, COIN operations are tremendously costly. … The United States cannot afford such costly long term commitments.

Second, current levels of debt are unsustainable—failure to reign in the deficit will decimate the economy and cripple U.S. foreign policy.
Roger C. Altman, Chair and CEO of Evercore Partners, former U.S. Deputy Treasury Secretary, holds an A.B. from Georgetown University and an M.B.A. from the University of Chicago, and Richard N. Haass, President of the Council on Foreign Relations, former Director of Policy Planning at the U.S. State Department, recipient of the State Department's Distinguished Service Award, Vice President and Director of Foreign Policy Studies at the Brookings Institution, Sol M. Linowitz Visiting Professor of International Studies at Hamilton College, senior associate at the Carnegie Endowment for International Peace, Lecturer in Public Policy at Harvard University's Kennedy School of Government, and research associate at the International Institute for Strategic Studies, 11-01-2010 (“American Profligacy and American Power,” Foreign Affairs, November/December, Available Online at http://www.foreignaffairs.com/articles/66778/roger-c-altman-and-richard-n-haass/american-profligacy-and-american-power, Accessed 11-18-2010)
The U.S. government is incurring debt at a historically unprecedented and ultimately unsustainable rate. … There will be profound consequences, not just for Americans' standard of living but also for U.S. foreign policy and the coming era of international relations.

Third, this will decimate the U.S. economic model—reigning in the deficit is vital to restore American credibility.
Roger C. Altman, Chair and CEO of Evercore Partners, former U.S. Deputy Treasury Secretary, holds an A.B. from Georgetown University and an M.B.A. from the University of Chicago, and Richard N. Haass, President of the Council on Foreign Relations, former Director of Policy Planning at the U.S. State Department, recipient of the State Department's Distinguished Service Award, Vice President and Director of Foreign Policy Studies at the Brookings Institution, Sol M. Linowitz Visiting Professor of International Studies at Hamilton College, senior associate at the Carnegie Endowment for International Peace, Lecturer in Public Policy at Harvard University's Kennedy School of Government, and research associate at the International Institute for Strategic Studies, 11-01-2010 (“American Profligacy and American Power,” Foreign Affairs, November/December, Available Online at http://www.foreignaffairs.com/articles/66778/roger-c-altman-and-richard-n-haass/american-profligacy-and-american-power, Accessed 11-18-2010)
A related cost of the United States' debt has even greater consequences: the diminished appeal of the American model of market-based capitalism. … The result of reduced U.S. power will be a world that is messier and, in the end, less safe and less prosperous.

And, this is the comparatively greatest threat to U.S. security.
Justin Raimondo, Editorial Director of Antiwar.com, contributing editor for The American Conservative, senior fellow at the Randolph Bourne Institute, Adjunct Scholar with the Ludwig von Mises Institute, 11-04-2010 (“The Biggest Threat to America,” AntiWar.com, November 4th, Available Online at http://original.antiwar.com/justin/2010/11/04/the-biggest-threat-to-america/print/, Accessed 11-12-2010)
Whenever you go to an airport, you hear an announcement about the “terror alert”: it’s yellow, it’s red, it’s orange, whatever. … The irony is that the former will be the undoing of the latter: the destruction of the dollar means the implosion of the American empire, and the relegation of the US to what used to be called – in more politically incorrect times – a “Third World” nation.

The nature of the current economic decline is dependent upon whether or not the U.S. model survives—the impact is global poverty, disease, terrorism, and armed conflicts.
Joseph S. Nye, Jr., Professor at Harvard University and former Dean of the Harvard Kennedy School, 2009 (“The worst of all worlds,” Taipei Times, April 13th, Available Online at http://www.taipeitimes.com/News/editorials/archives/2009/04/13/2003440899, Accessed 05-03-2009)
The world economy will shrink this year for the first time since 1945, and some economists worry that the current crisis could spell the beginning of the end of globalization. … So, unless governments cooperate to stimulate their economies and resist protectionism, the world may find that the current economic crisis does not mean the end of globalization, but only the end of the good kind, leaving us with the worst of all worlds.

Fortunately, shifting to a counterterrorism posture solves.
Roger C. Altman, Chair and CEO of Evercore Partners, former U.S. Deputy Treasury Secretary, holds an A.B. from Georgetown University and an M.B.A. from the University of Chicago, and Richard N. Shrinking budgets are likely to have an even greater impact on the future of the U.S. role in Afghanistan. … They suggest a more modest counterterrorism approach: going after the terrorists directly with drones, cruise missiles, and special forces, much as the United States is doing in Yemen and Somalia.

Plan:

The United States federal government should draw down the population-centric counterinsurgency presence of its military in Afghanistan to 68,000 or less troops conducting counterterrorism operations by October 2011 and 30,000 or less troops conducting counterterrorism operations by July 2012.

Contention Three is Progressivism

Engaging in a policy debate over alternatives to the COIN strategy in Afghanistan is vital to real world change—their alternative is hopelessly out of touch.
Michael A. Cohen, former Senior Research Fellow at the New America Foundation, B.A. in International Relations from American University and M.A. in International Relations from Columbia University, served in the Department of State as chief speechwriter for U.S. Representative to the United Nations Bill Richardson and Undersecretary of State Stuart Eizenstat, currently serves on the board of the National Security Network and has taught at Columbia University’s School of International and Public Affairs, 2010 (“Hard Is Not Hopeless: Affecting Outcomes in Afghanistan,” Democracy Arsenal—an international relations blog published by the Center for American Progress and the Century Foundation's Security and Peace Initiative, July 30th, Available Online at http://progressiverealist.org/blogpost/hard-not-hopeless-affecting-outcomes-afghanistan, Accessed 09-09-2010)
Second, it oddly disregards the shifting national debate on Afghanistan. … The notion that a critic of the current policy would throw up their hands and argue that all is lost; let's worry about stopping the next humanitarian intervention is both ill-advised and ill-informed.

Ignoring the policy debate about Afghanistan dooms progressivism for the next three decades—their alternative is politically suicidal.
Lorelei Kelly, national security expert and Director of the New Strategic Security Initiative and the Afghanistan Congressional Communications Hub, former Senior Associate at the Henry L. Stimson Center, holds a B.A. from Grinnell College and an M.A. from Stanford University and the Air Command and Staff College of the U.S. Air Force, 2010 (“Afghanistan: Should We Stay or Should We Go?,” Foreign Policy In Focus, April 14th, Available Online at http://www.fpif.org/articles/afghanistan_should_we_stay_or_should_we_go, Accessed 09-09-2010)
I think the greatest question that we have as Americans is, are we going to put everything on the table and talk about what works in today's world. … Because the future of U.S. national security is being decided right now: How Afghanistan turns out, even if it doesn't turn out in a way that any of us hoped for, is going to define security policy-making for the next generation.

Debating about exit strategies is vitally important—the alternative cedes policy discussions to the military elite.
Michael A. Cohen, former Senior Research Fellow at the New America Foundation, B.A. in International Relations from American University and M.A. in International Relations from Columbia University, served in the Department of State as chief speechwriter for U.S. Representative to the United Nations Bill Richardson and Undersecretary of State Stuart Eizenstat, currently serves on the board of the National Security Network and has taught at Columbia University’s School of International and Public Affairs, 2010 (“Hard Is Not Hopeless: Affecting Outcomes in Afghanistan,” Democracy Arsenal—an international relations blog published by the Center for American Progress and the Century Foundation's Security and Peace Initiative, July 30th, Available Online at http://progressiverealist.org/blogpost/hard-not-hopeless-affecting-outcomes-afghanistan, Accessed 09-09-2010)
But the issue in Afghanistan is not whether we go in - it's how we get out. … That's why a national debate about Afghanistan right NOW is so important; because it will hopefully offer ideas for how we can get out while also leaving our interests protected and the Afghan state in as stable a place as possible.

Totalizing rejection of the military is a failing strategy—it cedes the policy debate to conservatives.
Lorelei Kelly, national security expert and Director of the New Strategic Security Initiative and the Afghanistan Congressional Communications Hub, former Senior Associate at the Henry L. Stimson Center, holds a B.A. from Grinnell College and an M.A. from Stanford University and the Air Command and Staff College of the U.S. Air Force, 2010 (“Afghanistan: Should We Stay or Should We Go?,” Foreign Policy In Focus, April 14th, Available Online at http://www.fpif.org/articles/afghanistan_should_we_stay_or_should_we_go, Accessed 09-09-2010)
What I'm saying is that if you have a message that always makes the military a malign actor, I think that you are not going to get inside the room on the policy debate. … I fear that if progressives aren't gently and lovingly making the case that the military is not the vehicle to carry out the policies that are going to make us more prosperous and secure in the future, we are going to be a country where the only functioning, healthy institution is the military.

Contention Two: U.S. Military Power

The counterinsurgency mission in Afghanistan is undermining the strength of U.S. military power—we’ll introduce several internal links.

First, dogmatic commitment to COIN creates an expectations gap and ensures serial policy failure.
Gian P. Gentile, Assistant Professor of History and Director of the Military History Program at the United States Military Academy, Lieutenant Colonel in the U.S. Army who has served two tours of duty in Iraq, Visiting Fellow at the Council on Foreign Relations, 2010 (“Freeing the Army from the Counterinsurgency Straightjacket,” Joint Force Quarterly, Issue 58, July, Available Online at http://ndu.edu/press/counterinsurgency-straightjacket.html, Accessed 08-31-2010)
Carl von Clausewitz said that a center of gravity is something to be discovered, and it could vary depending on the aims of the war being fought.3 … Perhaps the Army needs to find the time to do it now.

Second, COIN operations in Afghanistan have crippled overall readiness by eliminating training exercises, crowding out preparation for major conflicts, impoverishing equipment inventories, and depleting U.S. strategic reserves.
David Wood, Chief Military Correspondent for Politics Daily, has worked as a war reporter for 30 years, was named a Pulitzer Prize finalist for his reporting on conflict, national security, and foreign affairs, has won the Gerald Ford Prize for Distinguished Reporting on National Defense and the Headliners Club award for his reporting on Iraq, has lectured at the Marine Staff College, the Joint Forces Staff College, and the Army's Eisenhower Fellows conference, 12-27-2010 (“Busy With Afghanistan, the U.S. Military Has No Time to Train for Big Wars,” Politics Daily, December 27th, Available Online at http://www.politicsdaily.com/2010/12/27/busy-with-afghanistan-the-u-s-military-has-no-time-to-train-fo/print/, Accessed 01-03-2011)
The painful story of Task Force Smith is once again being told amid growing anxiety that the United States is so focused on today's missions that it has neglected to prepare for what may come next. … Senior Marine officers often lament that a decade into counterinsurgency operations, the Corps has midcareer officers and non-commissioned officers who have never been on a ship, let alone learned the complex art of amphibious operations, the Marines' central mission.

Third, COIN narrows the gap between the U.S. and its peer competitors—this negates the benefits of strong military power.
Bernard I. Finel, Professor of Military Strategy and Operations at the U.S. National War College, former Executive Director of the Security Studies Program and the Center for Peace and Security Studies at Georgetown University, 2010 (“An alternative to COIN,” Armed Forces Journal, February, Available Online at http://www.afji.com/2010/02/4387134, Accessed 10-12-2010)
The U.S. military is a dominant fighting force, capable of rapid global power projection and able to defeat state adversaries quickly and at relatively low cost in American lives and treasure. … Asymmetric “threats” are self-limited and should be largely ignored, and by doing so, the U.S. could free itself to apply military power more effectively in the types of conflict where American forces excel.

Fourth, the status quo is the worst of all possible worlds: attempting to maintain a full-spectrum military while simultaneously engaging in COIN will decimate U.S. military power and provoke the rise of hostile global rivals.
Daniel Goure, Vice President of the Lexington Institute, former Director of the Office of Strategic Competitiveness in the Office of the Secretary of Defense, former Deputy Director of the International Security Program at the Center for Strategic and International Studies, holds an M.A. and Ph.D. International Relations and Russian Studies from Johns Hopkins University and a B.A. in Government and History from Pomona College, 2010 (“Future Defense Budget Choices Require Clear Strategic Priorities,” Early Warning Blog—the official blog of the Lexington Institute, September 3rd, Available Online at http://www.lexingtoninstitute.org/future-defense-budget-choices-require-clear-strategic-priorities?a=1&c=1171, Accessed 09-08-2010)
The United States cannot afford its national security strategy. … The consequence of this means focusing more on capabilities related to air and sea power, outer space and cyber space.

The impact is the breakdown of the international system—only a strong U.S. military can deter hostile global rivals and prevent global conflict.
Alvin S. Felzenberg, Professorial Lecturer at The Elliott School of International Affairs at George Washington University, Presidential Historian and Adjunct Faculty Member at the Annenberg School for Communication at the University of Pennsylvania, former Fellow at the Institute of Politics at the John F. Kennedy School of Government at Harvard University, served as Principal Spokesman for the 9/11 Commission, holds a Ph.D. in Politics from Princeton University, and Alexander B. Gray, Student at the Elliott School of International Affairs at George Washington University and the War Studies Department of King’s College, London, 01-03-2011 (“The New Isolationism,” The National Review, January 3rd, Available Online at http://www.nationalreview.com/articles/print/256150, Accessed 01-03-2011)
Anything Reps. Ron Paul (R., Tex.) and Barney Frank (D., Mass.) both support should give the rest of us pause. … Congress and the president would do well to reflect on those lessons and remember their duty to provide a dominant American military presence on land, at sea, and in the air.

A strong military is crucial to spur the international cooperation needed to address emerging threats—failure to do so makes global conflict inevitable.
The Quadrennial Defense Review Independent Panel—a bipartisan congressional panel charged with conducting an assessment of the assumptions, strategy, findings, and risks described in the Department of Defense’s Quadrennial Defense Review, 2010 (“The QDR in Perspective: Meeting America’s National Security Needs In the 21st Century,” The Final Report of the Quadrennial Defense Review Independent Panel, Released on July 29th, Available Online at http://www.usip.org/files/qdr/qdrreport.pdf, Accessed 11-12-2010, p. 28-29)
These five trends frame a range of choices for U.S. government and Department of Defense planners. … It will simply lead to an increasingly unstable and [end page 28] unfriendly global climate and, eventually, to conflicts America cannot ignore, which we must prosecute with limited choices under unfavorable circumstances—and with stakes that are higher than anyone would like.

Prioritize this impact—great power conflict is the only existential risk.
Paul K. Chisholm, foreign policy and international relations scholar based in Kenya, holds a University Degree in Sociology and Diploma in Human Resources, 2005 (“Terrorism, the Threat in Perspective and Great Power Conflict,” Free World Syndicate, April, Available Online at http://www.synd.org/opinion-columns-chisholm/terrorism.html, Accessed 09-08-2010)
We've heard much about terrorism since the 9/11 attacks. … Diplomacy should be redirected to head off a full scale arms race and potential conflicts between nuclear parties.

And, the plan is the best way to maintain American hegemony—it maximizes deterrence while abandoning foolish and ineffective adventurism.
Christopher Layne, Associate Professor in the Bush School of Government and Public Service at Texas A&M University and Research Fellow with the Center on Peace and Liberty at The Independent Institute, 2006 (“America cannot rely on power alone,” Financial Times, August 24th, Available Online at http://www.ft.com/cms/s/f7bb5fb2-330c-11db-87ac-0000779e2340.html, Accessed 04-12-2007)
Hegemony, however, is not omnipotence. … If the US is to be perceived as powerful rather than powerless, it must refrain from intervening abroad in pursuit of unrealisable goals, and will have to learn that the intricacies of international politics cannot be reduced to a simplistic Manichean struggle between good and evil.

Finally, the plan is vital to reorient U.S. foreign and defense policy—a drawdown in Afghanistan will shore up American power.
Richard N. Haass, President of the Council on Foreign Relations, 12-30-2010 (“Endgames in Iraq and Afghanistan,” The Japan Times, December 30th, Available Online at http://search.japantimes.co.jp/cgi-bin/eo20101230a2.html, Accessed 01-03-2011)
And it is difficult to be optimistic about Afghanistan, given the Taliban's resilience, the weakness and corruption that plague the government, and the reality that Pakistan will continue to be a sanctuary for the Taliban and other armed groups seeking to gain a foothold (or more) in Afghanistan. … It is in the interest of the U.S. and the world that this opportunity not be missed.

Plan:

The United States federal government should draw down the population-centric counterinsurgency presence of its military in Afghanistan to 68,000 or less troops conducting counterterrorism operations by October 2011 and 30,000 or less troops conducting counterterrorism operations by July 2012.

Contention Three: Solvency

First, the plan’s shift to counter-terrorism is the most effective strategy—it reduces casualties, reassures allies, and keeps the pressure on extremists.
Thomas Snodgrass, Retired Colonel in the U.S. Air Force, Director of Military Affairs for The Society of Americans for National Existence, Former Adjunct Professor of History at Embry-Riddle Aeronautical University, 8-05-2010 (“Conservatives: Abandoning Counterinsurgency,” The American Thinker, August 5th, Available Online at http://www.americanthinker.com/2010/08/conservatives_abandoning_count.html, Accessed 08-30-2010)
The lesson learned from history must be to do what we know to be doable and to avoid what we have learned is not. … A "stable" Afghan government under Hamid Karzai is a losing proposition that should be carefully abandoned as inconspicuously and quickly as possible.

Finally, the plan is a substantial reduction in military presence.
The Afghanistan Study Group—an ad hoc group of public policy practitioners, former U.S. government officials, academics, business representatives, policy-concerned activists and association leaders concerned with the Obama administration’s policy course in Afghanistan, 8-16-2010 (“The Way Forward: A Five-Point Approach,” A New Way Forward: Rethinking U.S. Strategy in Afghanistan, August 16th, Available Online at http://www.afghanistanstudygroup.org/NewWayForward_report.pdf, Accessed 08-29-2010)
2. Scale back and eventually Suspend Combat operations in the South and Reduce the U.S. Military footprint. … This step would save the U.S. at least $60 billion to $80 billion per year and reduce local resentment at our large and intrusive military presence.

Monsters to Destroy


1NC Monsters K



Subpoint A: The Link
The affirmative's depiction of an imminent terrorist threat transforms the real people who commit terrorist acts into "the terr-ists," a group of shadowy monsters who are always just around the corner plotting their next attack. This framing of the terrorist threat sidesteps the question of why they hate us and normalizes a state of permanent national insecurity.
Ira Chernus, Professor of Religious Studies and Co-Director of the Peace and Conflict Studies Program at the University of Colorado-Boulder, 2006 (Monsters to Destroy: The Neoconservative War on Terror and Sin, Published by Paradigm Publishers, ISBN 1594512752, p. 1-2)
Monsters are not real—unless you believe in them AND for yet another round of attack and counterattack.

Subpoint B: The Impact
First—their impact claims are self-fulfilling – constant repetition of terrorism scenarios creates a vicious cycle of fear and violence.
Robert Jay Lifton, Visiting Professor of Psychiatry at Harvard Medical School, previously Distinguished Professor of Psychiatry and Psychology at the Graduate School and Director of The Center on Violence and Human Survival at John Jay College of Criminal Justice at the City University of New York, 2003 (Superpower Syndrome: America’s Apocalyptic Confrontation With The World, Published by Thunder’s Mouth Press / Nation Books, ISBN 1560255129, p. 115-116)
The amorphousness of the war on terrorism carries with it AND and act in concert with the Islamist apocalyptic.

Second—this turns the case – the affirmative’s framing of the terrorist threat entrenches a cycle of insecurity that makes attacks more likely.
Ira Chernus, Professor of Religious Studies and Co-Director of the Peace and Conflict Studies Program at the University of Colorado-Boulder, 2006 (Monsters to Destroy: The Neoconservative War on Terror and Sin, Published by Paradigm Publishers, ISBN 1594512752, p. 4-5)
The structure is simple enough. A battle is raging AND the United States will suffer another grievous attack.

Subpoint C: The Alternative
The alternative is to problematize the affirmative’s impact claims and stop believing in monsters. This is not a counterplan – our contention is that the affirmative’s policy prescription is inescapably bound up in the stories they tell to justify action. The role of the ballot should be to choose between two competing visions of the world – we advocate a fundamentally different methodological approach that is mutually exclusive with that of the affirmative.
Ira Chernus, Professor of Religious Studies and Co-Director of the Peace and Conflict Studies Program at the University of Colorado-Boulder, 2006 (Monsters to Destroy: The Neoconservative War on Terror and Sin, Published by Paradigm Publishers, ISBN 1594512752, p. 11-13)
The twin towers of the World Trade Center are goneAND certainty, and the difference between the two.

TOC - 1NC Shell


Death and misfortune are inevitable – the joy of life comes in accepting things as they are – plans to the world only bring more suffering
Slabbert, 01 [Jos, Taoist teacher and philosopher, “Tao te Ching: How to Deal with Suffering” http://www.taoism.net/theway/suffer.htm]
Dealing with loss Express yourself completely, then keep quiet. Be like the forces of nature: when it blows, there is only wind; when it rains, there is only rain; when the clouds pass, the sun shines through. If you open yourself to the Tao, you are at one with the Tao and you can embody it completely. If you open yourself to insight, you are at one with insight and you can use it completely. If you open yourself to loss, you are at one with loss and you can accept it completely. Open yourself to the Tao, then trust your natural responses; and everything will fall into place. (Chapter 23) The word "open" is repeated often in this poem. Most people think the only way to handle suffering is to withdraw and to close yourself. The poet is clearly saying in this poem that the opposite is true: If you open yourself to loss, you are at one with loss and you can accept it completely. This openness, a willingness and courage to face reality, is the only way to deal with suffering, particularly inescapable suffering. But the openness the poet is describing is more than just facing reality. It is facing reality in total harmony with the Tao: If you open yourself to the Tao, you are at one with the Tao and you can embody it completely. It is only when you "embody" the Tao that you can face suffering with true equanimity. You will then have the openness that insight into your own nature and the natural way of Tao brings you. The right approach to suffering is only possible when you have reduced your ego to a minimum. The less ego you have, the less you suffer. Facing death with unresolved agendas is a terrible form of suffering. You will have to let go of selfish interests and futile aims to concentrate on dealing with the moment. It is the acceptance of the inevitable that makes suffering bearable. On his death bed, his family mourning, he is serene, for he knows Death, like Life, is an illusion: there is no beginning and no end. There is only the endless flow of Tao. The man of Tao has no fear, for he walks with Tao. (The Tao is Tao, 154) Agendas A good traveler has no fixed plans and is not intent upon arriving. (Chapter 27) Plans, aims, objectives and agendas have become the routes of suffering for so many people, and not only the ambitious. Agendas often take spontaneity and joy out of life. In the process, many people have become bad travelers, concentrating only on their objectives, and arriving at their destinations only to find that even their destinations are not really worth the trouble. Having no fixed plans? This does not sound like survival in a modern technological environment, does it? I mean, who but the extremely fortunate have the luxury of not having agendas running their lives? In most cases, one could justifiably point out, agendas are forced on you by your professional and familial obligations. You do not really have a choice, do you? How could one then become a good traveler through life in this modern world? I think the key lies in the second line of the quotation. One should not be "intent upon arriving". You should adopt an attitude of detachment. The moment your aims become egocentric, your suffering increases. The less your own ego is involved, the less seriously you will take life, and the more you will enjoy the journey. It is easier said than done, though, particularly when the job you are doing seems to be devoid of meaning, and the activities on your agenda tedious. They might even go against what you truly believe. It is clear. To become a good traveler in the modern world often entails more than just a change of attitude. It could also mean changing your life style, even your profession. It could mean taking risks in the process. But liberation has always been a risky undertaking, hasn’t it? People are willing to take risks for the most mundane things like profit and possession. Why not take a few risks when your spiritual progress is at stake? Truly good travelers often leave the beaten track and become masters of their own far more adventurous journeys. Tampering with the world Do you want to improve the world? I don't think it can be done. The world is sacred. It can't be improved. If you tamper with it, you'll ruin it. If you treat it like an object, you'll lose it. (Chapter 29) If anything, the Twentieth Century will be called the century of social engineering. Simplistic ideologies, like fascism, were used to try to change the world, with terrible consequences inducing suffering on a scale never seen before in the history of the human being. A savage economic system based on greed - capitalism - has ravaged the world. Yet, the human being has not learnt from this. Still, politicians show their ignorance by tampering with the sacred. It is the age of management, that euphemistic word for manipulating society. It is still happening. What else are many political programs but tampering with the sacred and ruining it in the process? It is the source of endless suffering. Forcing issues Whoever relies on the Tao in governing men doesn't try to force issues or defeat enemies by force of arms. For every force there is a counterforce. Violence, even well intentioned, always rebounds upon oneself. The Master does his job and then stops. He understands that the universe is forever out of control, and that trying to dominate events goes against the current of the Tao. (Chapter 30) Understanding that the universe is out of control is the key to wisdom and patience. No amount of tampering with the universe will change this. In fact, the more we tamper with it, the more damage we will do.

The 1AC’s rationalist attempt to anticipate the effects of foreign policy will fail. Emptying oneself of being is a prerequisite to true understanding.
Ralph Pettman, Professor in the School of History, Philosophy, Political Science and International Relations at Victoria University of Wellington, 2005 (“Taoism And The Concept Of Global Security,” International Relations of the Asia-Pacific, Volume 5, Number 1, Available Online to Subscribing Institutions via Oxford Journals Online, p. 72-75)
How does moving onto Taoist ground, and immersing ourselves in Taoist experience, play out in practice? Our section on mapping the concept of global security began by highlighting the making of modernity. If we start with this general project, and cast it in the light of the general Taoist knack for sacral spontaneity, we see at once how little this knack has to do with the rationalist way of thinking or being. Where the modernist/rationalist talks of empirical logic and scientific representation, the sacralist/Taoist talks (in Graham’s terms, at least) of the rejection of empirical logic, and an ‘infinite regress, testing by tests which in the end are themselves untested’ (Graham, [end page 72] 1981, pp. 10, 11). Where the rationalist talks of the hypothetico-​deductive method, the Taoist talks (again in Graham’s terms) of an understanding of the ‘mysterious order which runs through all things’, and the universal motion of chi energy (Graham, 1981, pp. 12, 19–20). Where the rationalist talks of a detached and individuated intellectual vantage-​point, separate from society, where reason can be given free reign to cogitate and communicate, the Taoist talks (in Hansen’s terms this time) of ‘heart-​minds’ (Hansen, 1992, pp. 53, 85–86). Taoists respond to the situation they are in by unfocusing, that is, by allowing themselves to act with the ‘immediacy of an echo’, rather than the self-​consciousness of someone who applies general principles. (Graham, 1981, pp. 6, 12, 14). They invite, in other words, the kind of recognition the ‘heart’ gives ‘when the mind is silent’ (Krishnamurti, 1972, p. 34). This is metaphorical language, but we are not, after all, trying to ascertain what is scientifically true. We are trying to locate scientific truth-​finding within its sacral context. 8 Faced with global security planning, Taoists highlight the way rationalist attempts to anticipate a particular foreign policy can only reach so far. Taoists highlight how those who really know what they are doing tend to eschew conscious thought to attend instead to the ‘total situation’. This ‘knack’, like a feel for the way a bacterium works, or for how to play a musical instrument, is not one that can be ultimately explained (Graham, 1983, p. 7). Taoists also compare the way they face the future with the way it is faced by those who promote the national interest, for example, or the relevant capitalist/corporate, politico-​social, bourgeois, or masculinist interest. The rationalist entertains options A, B, and C, and plays out each one in advance, in a bid to anticipate what will turn out the best. Except that it is not possible to anticipate what will turn out the best. In choosing one policy option, the others cease to exist. Once, for example, option B is chosen, options A and C have no chance of happening. Option A might have been better, or might have been worse. Likewise option C. With the B policy chosen, these alternatives are no longer alternatives. Which is why contemplating such alternatives was futile in the first place, and making decisions on the basis of such contemplations makes no sense at all. It is not possible, that is, to know rationally what is in the national interest. To rely on rationalism is, therefore, to overplay rationalism, which is to underplay Taoism in turn, and to underplay sacral spontaneity (Graham, 1981, p. 14). [end page 73] Modernist proponents of global security demur. Enough people in the world live as if modernist conceptions of global security ought to prevail, they note, for most of these conceptions to prevail in practice. Enough people behave as if world order is made up of sovereign states, for example, for this way of ordering world affairs to be a tangible, global reality. The same applies to liberal marketeering, global modes of making civil identity, the global formation of capitalist classes, the global advent of social movements, and the global advent of gender-​specific practices. There is a self-​fulfilling quality to the modernist project, and we must deal with its global consequences, they argue, whether the Taoist critique of the rationalist cause is valid or not. This is not to say that the people of the world live in the best of all possible worlds. Perhaps there is a preferred alternative, though perhaps (and this is the Taoist thought) there is no ‘preferred alternative’ either, at least of a rationally accessible sort. Perhaps it is a matter of standing back to look at this cosmos that we are all in, then standing close to listen, then feeling as best we can for how it moves, before standing back to look once more. Perhaps we might even learn something in taking ourselves through such a process, something we might need to know if we are to understand global security.

No one knows what is good and bad. Reject the aff’s judgments, even if we lose all life on earth
Kirkland, 98 [Russell Kirkland, Associate Professor of Religion (and Asian Studies), “"Responsible Non-Action" In a Natural World: Perspectives from the Nei-Yeh, Chuang-Tzu, and Tao-Te Ching,” 1998, University of Georgia, http://kirkland.myweb.uga.edu/rk/pdf/pubs/ECO.pdf]
